Ernst, and Lu Zhang\nAbstract—The performance of fault localization techniques is critical to their adoption in practice. This paper reports on an empirical\nstudy of a wide range of fault localization techniques on real-world faults. Different from previous studies, this paper (1) considers a\nwide range of techniques from different families, (2) combines different techniques, and (3) considers the execution time of different\ntechniques. Our results reveal that a combined technique signiﬁcantly outperforms any individual technique (200% increase in faults\nlocalized in Top 1), suggesting that combination may be a desirable way to apply fault localization techniques and that future\ntechniques should also be evaluated in the combined setting. Our implementation is publicly available for evaluating and combining\nfault localization techniques.\nIndex Terms—Fault localization, learning to rank, program debugging, software testing, empirical study.\n!\n1\nINTRODUCTION\nT\nHE goal of fault localization is to identify defective pro-\ngram elements related to software failures. Automated\nfault localization uses static and run-time information about\nthe program to identify program elements that may be the\nroot cause of the failure. This paper considers seven families\nof fault localization techniques, which take as input seven\ndifferent types of information:\n•\nSpectrum-based fault localization (SBFL) [1], [2], [3]:\nutilizing test coverage information\n•\nMutation-based fault localization (MBFL) [4], [5]:\nutilizing test results from mutating the program\n•\nDynamic program slicing [6], [7]: utilizing dynamic\nprogram dependencies\n•\nStack trace analysis [8], [9]: utilizing crash reports\n•\nPredicate switching [10]: utilizing test results from\nmutating the results of conditional expressions\n•\nInformation-retrieval-based fault localization (IR-\nbased FL) [11]: utilizing bug report information\n•\nHistory-based fault localization [12], [13]: utilizing\nthe development history\nSome techniques compute a suspiciousness score for\neach program element and can generate a ranked list of\nelements, such as spectrum-based fault localization. Other\ntechniques only mark a set of elements as suspicious, such\nas dynamic program slicing.\nThe performance of fault localization is critical to its\nadoption in practice. A number\nof empirical studies [17], [18], [19], [20] have evaluated the\nperformance of SBFL and MBFL. When using a\ntechnique at one time cost level, it does not affect\nrun time to include all techniques from the preceding\nlevels, but it does improve fault localization effective-\nness.\n•\nThe above ﬁndings hold at both statement and\nmethod granularities — that is, when the FL tech-\nnique is identifying suspicious statements and when\nit is identifying suspicious methods.\nTo sum up, the paper makes the following contributions.\n•\nThe ﬁrst empirical study that compares a wide range\nof fault localization techniques on real faults.\n•\nA combined technique, CombineFL, which is con-\nﬁgurable based on the time cost, and the peak per-\nformance of the technique signiﬁcantly outperforms\nstandalone techniques.\n•\nAn infrastructure, CombineFL-core, for evaluating\nand combining fault localization techniques for fu-\nture research.\nThe rest of the paper is organized as follows. +Choice, -Action)\n    Dynamic predicate, normally not defined. This predicate is called from the SWI-Prolog debugger just before it would show a port. If this predicate succeeds, the debugger assumes that the trace action has been taken care of and continues execution as described by Action. Otherwise the normal Prolog debugger actions are performed. \n\nPort denotes the reason to activate the tracer (`port’in the 4/5-port, but with some additions): \n\n**call**\n     Normal entry through the call port of the 4-port debugger.\n**redo**(PC)\n    Normal entry through the redo port of the 4-port debugger. The `redo` port signals resuming a predicate to generate alternative solutions. If PC is 0 (zero), clause indexing has found another clause that will be tried next. Otherwise, PC is the program counter in the current clause where execution continues. This implies we are dealing with an in-clause choice point left by, e.g., [;/2](/pldoc/man?predicate=%3B/2). Note that non-determinism in foreign predicates are also handled using an in-clause choice point.\n**unify**\n     The unify port represents the _neck_ instruction, signalling the end of the head-matching process. This port is normally invisible. See [leash/1](/pldoc/man?predicate=leash/1) and [visible/1](/pldoc/man?predicate=visible/1).\n**exit**\n     The exit port signals the goal is proved. It is possible for the goal to have alternatives. See [prolog_frame_attribute/3](/pldoc/man?predicate=prolog_frame_attribute/3) to examine the goal stack.\n**fail**\n     The fail port signals final failure of the goal.\n**exception**(Except)\n    An exception is raised and still pending. This port is activated on each parent frame of the frame generating the exception until the exception is caught or the user restarts normal computation using `retry`. Except is the pending exception term.\n**cut_call**(PC)\n    A cut is encountered at PC. This port is used by the graphical debugger to visualise the effect of the cut.\n**cut_exit**(PC)\n    A cut has been executed. See `cut_call(PC)` for more information.\n\nFrame is a reference to the current local stack frame, which can be examined using [prolog_frame_attribute/3](/pldoc/man?predicate=prolog_frame_attribute/3). Choice is a reference to the last choice point and can be examined using [prolog_choice_attribute/3](/pldoc/man?predicate=prolog_choice_attribute/3). Action must be unified with a term that specifies how execution must continue. The following actions are defined: \n\n**abort**\n     Abort execution. See [abort/0](/pldoc/man?predicate=abort/0).\n**halt**\n     Terminate execution. See [halt/0](/pldoc/man?predicate=halt/0).\n**continue**\n     Continue (i.e., _creep_ in the command line debugger).\n**fail**\n     Make the current goal fail.\n**ignore**\n     Step over the current goal without executing it.\n**nodebug**\n     Continue execution in normal nodebug mode. See [nodebug/0](/pldoc/man?predicate=nodebug/0).\n**leap**\n     Continue execution in normal debug mode. See [debug/0](/pldoc/man?predicate=debug/0).\n**retry**\n     Retry the current frame.\n**retry**(Frame)\n    Retry the given frame. This must be a parent of the current frame.\n**skip**\n     Skip over the current goal (i.e., _skip_ in the command line debugger).\n**skip**(Frame)\n    Skip to the end the execution of Frame. This is used to implement _finish_ on an arbitrary frame in the GUI debugger.\n**up**\n     Skip to the parent goal (i.e., _up_ in the command line debugger). This is the same as `skip(Frame)` using the parent frame of the current frame.\n\nTogether with the predicates described in [section 4.39](/pldoc/man?section=debugger) and the other predicates of this chapter, this predicate enables the Prolog user to define a complete new debugger in Prolog. Besides this, it enables the Prolog programmer to monitor the execution of a program. The example below records all goals trapped by the tracer in the database. \n    \n    \n    prolog_trace_interception(Port, Frame, _PC, continue) :-\n            prolog_frame_attribute(Frame, goal, Goal),\n            prolog_frame_attribute(Frame, level, Level),\n            recordz(trace, trace(Port, Level, Goal)).\n\nTo trace the execution of‘go’this way the following query should", "stderr": "", "interrupted": false, "isImage": false, "noOutputExpected": false}

### [25] TOOL RESULT — Bash · 2026-06-07 05:00:04 UTC

{"stdout": "URL: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2305.12295\nType: PDF\nLength: 77184 chars (truncated)\n\n--- Content ---\n\nLOGIC-LM: Empowering Large Language Models with\nSymbolic Solvers for Faithful Logical Reasoning\nLiangming Pan\nAlon Albalak\nXinyi Wang\nWilliam Yang Wang\nUniversity of California, Santa Barbara\n{liangmingpan, alon_albalak, xinyi_wang, wangwilliamyang}@ucsb.edu\nAbstract\nLarge Language Models (LLMs) have shown\nhuman-like reasoning abilities but still strug-\ngle with complex logical problems. This pa-\nper introduces a novel framework, LOGIC-\nLM, which integrates LLMs with symbolic\nsolvers to improve logical problem-solving.\nOur method first utilizes LLMs to translate\na natural language problem into a symbolic\nformulation. Afterward, a deterministic sym-\nbolic solver performs inference on the for-\nmulated problem. We also introduce a self-\nrefinement module, which utilizes the symbolic\nsolver’s error messages to revise symbolic for-\nmalizations. We demonstrate LOGIC-LM’s ef-\nfectiveness on five logical reasoning datasets:\nProofWriter, PrOntoQA, FOLIO, LogicalDe-\nduction, and AR-LSAT. On average, LOGIC-\nLM achieves a significant performance boost\nof 39.2% over using LLM alone with standard\nprompting and 18.4% over LLM with chain-of-\nthought prompting. Our findings suggest that\nLOGIC-LM, by combining LLMs with sym-\nbolic logic, offers a promising avenue for faith-\nful logical reasoning. 1\n1\nIntroduction\nLogical reasoning is a cognitive process that in-\nvolves using evidence, arguments, and logic to ar-\nrive at conclusions or make judgments (Huang and\nChang, 2023). It plays a central role in intelligent\nsystems for problem-solving, decision-making, and\ncritical thinking. Recently, large language models\n(LLMs) (Brown et al., 2020; Ouyang et al., 2022a;\nOpenAI, 2023) have exhibited emergent ability to\n“reason” like human (Wei et al., 2022a). When\nprompted with step-wise explanations of reasoning\n(“chain of thoughts”), or a simple prompt “Let’s\nthink step by step.”, these models are able to an-\nswer questions with explicit reasoning steps (Wei\net al., 2022b; Kojima et al., 2022).\n1Code and data are publicly available at https://github.\ncom/teacherpeterpan/Logic-LLM.\nProblem \nFormulator \nSymbolic \nReasoner \nResult \nInterpreter \nSymbolic \nFormulation\nSymbolic \nResult\nProblem\nGoal\nAnswer\nSelf-\nRefine\nFigure 1: Overview of our LOGIC-LM framework.\nDespite the advances of LLMs, they still strug-\ngle with complex logical reasoning problems (Liu\net al., 2023b). Recent studies (Golovneva et al.,\n2023; Ribeiro et al., 2023b; Lyu et al., 2023) found\nthat LLMs occasionally make unfaithful reason-\ning, i.e., the derived conclusion does not follow\nthe previously generated reasoning chain. While\nchain-of-thought may imitate human reasoning pro-\ncesses, the fundamental nature of LLMs remains\nthat of black-box probabilistic models, lacking a\nmechanism to guarantee the faithfulness of reason-\ning (Shanahan, 2022). In contrast, symbolic infer-\nence engines, such as expert systems (Metaxiotis\net al., 2002), are faithful and transparent because\nthe reasoning is based on symbolic-represented\nknowledge and follows well-defined inference rules\nthat adhere to logical principles. The main obsta-\ncle is how to accurately translate a problem into\nsymbolic representations, considering the inherent\nambiguity and flexibility of natural language. This\nis precisely where LLMs excel, making LLMs a\npromising complement to symbolic solvers.\nThis drives our exploration of neuro-symbolic\nmethods that integrate LLMs with symbolic reason-\ning. As illustrated in Figure 1, we present LOGIC-\narXiv:2305.12295v2  [cs.CL]  19 Oct 2023\n\nLM, a novel framework that decomposes a logical\nreasoning problem into three stages: Problem For-\nmulation, Symbolic Reasoning, and Result Inter-\npretation. During problem formulation, an LLM\nconverts the natural language description of the\nproblem into an appropriate symbolic formulation,\nidentifying key entities, facts, and rules present\nin the problem statement. Subsequently, at the\nsymbolic reasoning stage, a deterministic symbolic\nsolver performs inference on the symbolic formula-\ntion. Lastly, a result interpreter explains the output\nand maps it to the correct answer. By incorporating\nLLMs with symbolic solvers, we can exploit the\nrobust natural language understanding capabilities\nof LLMs to precisely represent the problem using\nsymbolic representations, while also taking advan-\ntage of the logical faithfulness and transparency\noffered by symbolic solvers. To improve the accu-\nracy of the symbolic parsing, we also incorporate\nthe idea of self-refinement to iteratively revise the\ngenerated logical form using the error messages\nfrom the symbolic solver as feedback.\nWe showcase the adaptability and effective-\nness of LOGIC-LM on five logical reasoning\ndatasets: ProofWriter (Tafjord et al., 2021), PrOn-\ntoQA (Saparov and He, 2023), FOLIO (Han et al.,\n2022), AR-LSAT (Zhong et al., 2022), and the Log-\nicalDeduction dataset from BigBench (Srivastava\net al., 2022). These datasets cover a wide range of\nlogical reasoning problems, including:\n• Deductive Reasoning problems\n• First-Order Logic (FOL) reasoning problems\n• Constraint Satisfaction Problems (CSP)\n• Analytical Reasoning (AR) problems\nWe integrate four types of symbolic inference tools\ntailored to these problems: 1) logic programming\nengine that supports deductive reasoning through\nforward/backward chaining; 2) FOL inference en-\ngine that derives new conclusions based on FOL\nrules and facts, 3) constraint optimization engine\nthat provides solvers for CSP over finite domains,\nand 4) boolean satisfiability problem (SAT) solver\nthat solves analytical reasoning problems.\nOur evaluations show that the strategy of inte-\ngrating LLMs with symbolic solvers performs sig-\nnificantly better than purely relying on LLMs for\nlogical reasoning, with an average improvement\nof 39.2% over the standard prompting and 18.4%\nover the chain-of-thought prompting (§ 4.1). We\nalso find that LOGIC-LM becomes increasingly ef-\nfective as the required reasoning depth increases\n(§ 4.3). Finally, by analyzing the impact of self-\nrefinement, we highlight the effectiveness of incre-\nmentally revising symbolic formalizations when\ninteracting with the symbolic solver (§ 4.4).\n2\nRelated Work\nLanguage Models for Logical Reasoning.\nRe-\ncent works in adapting LLMs for logical reasoning\ntasks can be broadly categorized into two groups:\n1) fine-tuning approaches that optimize LLMs’ rea-\nsoning ability through fine-tuning or training spe-\ncialized modules (Clark et al., 2020; Tafjord et al.,\n2022; Yang et al., 2022), and 2) in-context learning\napproaches that design special prompts to elicit\nLLMs’ step-by-step reasoning capabilities. Typical\nmethods include chain-of-thought prompting (Wei\net al., 2022b; Wang et al., 2023) that generates ex-\nplanations before the final answer and the least-to-\nmost prompting (Zhou et al., 2023) that breaks the\nproblem down into simpler components that can\nbe solved individually. Both the above approaches\nperform reasoning directly over natural language\n(NL), providing greater flexibility than symbolic-\nbased reasoning. However, the intrinsic complexity\nand ambiguity of NL also bring undesired issues\nsuch as unfaithful reasoning and hallucinations.\nDifferent from prior works, we use symbolic\nlanguage as the basic unit of reasoning. This effec-\ntively transfers the burden of executing complex,\nprecise reasoning from LLMs to more reliable, in-\nterpretable external symbolic solvers. Simultane-\nously, we leverage the strong in-context learning\nability of LLMs to formulate the NL-based prob-\nlem into suitable symbolic representations, thus\nmaintaining the benefit of flexibility.\nAlthough prior works (Mao et al., 2019; Gupta\net al., 2020; Manhaeve et al., 2021; Cai et al., 2021;\nTian et al., 2022; Pryor et al., 2023) also propose\nneuro-symbolic methods to combine neural net-\nworks with symbolic reasoning, these methods suf-\nfer from limitations such as hand-crafted or spe-\ncialized module designs that are not easily gen-\neralizable, or brittleness due to the difficulty of\noptimization. In contrast, we propose a more gen-\neralizable framework that integrates modern LLMs\nwith symbolic logic without the need for training\nor designing complex problem-specific modules.\nTool-augmented Language Models.\nLanguage\nmodels have inherent limitations such as the inabil-\nity to access up-to-date information, take actions,\nor perform precise mathematical reasoning. {"stdout": "URL: https://aclanthology.org/2021.findings-acl.317.pdf\nType: PDF\nLength: 57933 chars (truncated)\n\n--- Content ---\n\nFindings of the Association for Computational Linguistics: ACL-IJCNLP 2021, pages 3621–3634\nAugust 1–6, 2021. ©2021 Association for Computational Linguistics\n3621\nProofWriter: Generating Implications, Proofs, and Abductive Statements\nover Natural Language\nOyvind Tafjord, Bhavana Dalvi Mishra, Peter Clark\nAllen Institute for AI, Seattle, WA\n{oyvindt,bhavanad,peterc}@allenai.org\nAbstract\nTransformers have been shown to emulate log-\nical deduction over natural language theories\n(logical rules expressed in natural language),\nreliably assigning true/false labels to candi-\ndate implications.\nHowever, their ability to\ngenerate implications of a theory has not yet\nbeen demonstrated, and methods for recon-\nstructing proofs of answers are imperfect. In\nthis work we show that a generative model,\ncalled ProofWriter, can reliably generate both\nimplications of a theory and the natural lan-\nguage proofs that support them. In particular,\niterating a 1-step implication generator results\nin proofs that are highly reliable, and represent\nactual model decisions (rather than post-hoc\nrationalizations).\nOn the RuleTaker dataset,\nthe accuracy of ProofWriter’s proofs exceed\nprevious methods by +9% absolute, and in a\nway that generalizes to proof depths unseen in\ntraining and on out-of-domain problems. We\nalso show that generative techniques can per-\nform a type of abduction with high precision:\nGiven a theory and an unprovable conclusion,\nidentify a missing fact that allows the conclu-\nsion to be proved, along with a proof. These re-\nsults signiﬁcantly improve the viability of neu-\nral methods for systematically reasoning over\nnatural language.1\n1\nIntroduction\nA fundamental goal for AI, dating back to its\nearliest years, is automated reasoning: the abil-\nity to draw valid conclusions from explicitly pro-\nvided knowledge (McCarthy, 1959). However, ap-\nproaches relying on expressing knowledge in a\nformal representation language have sometimes\nproved challenging (Musen and Van der Lei, 1988).\nRecent work on RuleTaker (Clark et al., 2020)\ndemonstrated a modern approach to this goal, in\nwhich transformers emulate deductive reasoning\n1Datasets available at https://allenai.org/data/proofwriter\nFigure 1: Given facts, rules, and a question all ex-\npressed in natural language, ProofWriter answers the\nquestion and generates a proof of the answer.\nover statements expressed in natural language, by\nreliably assigning true/false labels to candidate im-\nplications. However, simply assigning true/false\nlabels is limiting. For practical purposes, systems\nshould also generate proofs of those labels, so that\ntheir conclusions can be veriﬁed and a human-\nunderstandable rationale be produced.\nRecent work on PRover, by Saha et al. (2020),\nprovided ﬁrst results towards this goal, assembling\nproofs by ﬁrst classifying which facts, rules, and\nconnections should be in the proof tree then using\nan Integer Linear Programming (ILP) module to\nenforce consistency constraints. However, the gen-\n\n3622\nFigure 2: ProofWriter iteratively generates 1-step im-\nplications and their proofs, and adds implications back\ninto into the context for deeper reasoning. The step-\nwise proof fragments are assembled into full proofs of\nN-hop conclusions.\nerated proofs were imperfect, and there were no\nguarantees that the model “believed” the proofs that\nit was reciting, i.e., that its QA module would agree\nwith the steps shown in the proof. In this paper,\nwe adopt a different approach, based on generation\nrather than classiﬁcation. Our system, ProofWriter,\ngenerates proofs such as that shown in Figure 1 by\niteratively generating 1-hop inferences and their\n(simple) proofs, adding implications back into the\ncontext for deeper reasoning, and assembling more\ncomplex proofs from the 1-hop fragments (Fig-\nure 2). As the accuracy of 1-hop inference is highly\nreliable, the accuracy of deeper inference and their\nproofs is also high. This results in proofs that sub-\nstantially exceed the earlier method’s accuracy, and\nalso reﬂect the model’s internal decisions, rather\nthan a post-hoc rationalization (i.e., is a “faithful”\nproof (Subramanian et al., 2020)).\nThe generative approach also affords two other\nnew capabilities. First, ProofWriter generates im-\nplications that logically follow from a NL (natural\nlanguage) theory, allowing enumeration of conse-\nquences (rather than only assigning truth values to\npre-conjectured hypotheses). Second, we demon-\nstrate (a constrained form of) abduction: Given a\ntheory and an unprovable conclusion, identify a\nmissing fact (if any) that allows the conclusion to\nbe proved when added to the theory, plus its proof.\nWe evaluate our work on a collection of natural\nlanguage reasoning datasets, including the Rule-\nTaker datasets as well as several new variants. We\nachieve state-of-the-art results in proof generation,\nand strong new baselines for implication enumera-\ntion and abduction over natural language theories.\nOur contributions are thus:\n1. A new method for proof generation for logical\nreasoning over natural language, that obtains\nstate-of-the-art results and is faithful to the\nmodel’s internal decisions.\n2. A method and baseline results for generating\nlogical implications of statements in NL.\n3. A method and baseline results for performing\nabduction over natural language statements.\n4. New datasets to promote further research.\nThese results signiﬁcantly improve the viability of\nneural methods for formal reasoning over language.\n2\nRelated Work\nOur work builds on the RuleTaker line of research,\nin which transformers learn to emulate a deductive\nreasoning algorithm (Clark et al., 2020). Unlike\nother approaches to reasoning such as parsing to a\nformal language (Kamath and Das, 2019), imple-\nmenting a reasoning algorithm with neural compo-\nnents (Weber et al., 2019; Rockt¨aschel and Riedel,\n2017), or SAT solving (Selsam et al., 2019), these\ntransformers emulate reasoning over language di-\nrectly, bypassing a formal representation.\nPRover (Saha et al., 2020), mentioned earlier,\nwas the ﬁrst system to also produce proofs in\nthis context, although its post hoc approach meant\nthat proofs did not necessarily represent the ac-\ntual model decisions. Gontier et al. (2020) also\nexplored the generation of answers and proofs, but\nin the context of rule induction with (≈10) ﬁxed\nrules to induce. In contrast, ProofWriter generates\nproofs from explicit NL rules (which may differ\nfor each problem). Similarly, formal theorem prov-\ning has explored proving mathematical theorems\nfrom ﬁxed, fundamental axioms, e.g., (Polu and\nSutskever, 2020; Wang and Deng, 2020), while\nProofWriter performs inference with differing sets\nof rules expressed in natural language.\nOur work is also distinct from the large body\nof work on rationales and explanation. Work on\nrationales aims to identify sentences (or phrases)\nthat caused a model to make a particular decision,\nbut without an explanation of why that rationale led\nto the answer (the model’s reasoning is opaque),\ne.g., (DeYoung et al., 2019; Narang et al., 2020).\nSimilarly, work on explanations has sought to gen-\n\n3623\nProof (and Answer)\nCQ →AP\nGiven theory C and hypothesis fact Q, determine Q’s truth A and proof P (if any)\nEnumeration\nC →I1, ..., In\nGiven C, generate all implications Ii that logically follow.\nAbduction\nCQ →fm\nGiven C and an unprovable fact Q, identify a new fact fm that, when added to C,\nwould make Q true.\nTable 1: The three tasks that ProofWriter performs.\nerate human-style justiﬁcations, which again are\ntypically supporting evidence rather than a fully-\nformed line of reasoning, and without explicit\nreasoning rules (Camburu et al., 2018; Jhamtani\nand Clark, 2020; Inoue et al., 2020). In contrast,\nProofWriter produces a deductive chain of reason-\ning from what is known to what is concluded, using\na transformer retrained to reason systematically.\n3\nApproach\n3.1\nDeﬁnitions\nLet:\n• C be a theory, a set of English sentences C\nconsisting of facts F and rules R, each ex-\npressing a logical fact or rule in English. (We\nalso refer to C as the context).\n• Q be a question, a hypothesis fact in English\nwhose truth is to be determined based solely\non the information in C.\n• A be an answer, where A ∈{True, False}\n(if reasoning using a closed-world assump-\ntion) or A\n∈\n{True, False, Unknown}\n(open-world assumption).\n• P be a proof, described shortly.\n• I be an implication, a fact that logically fol-\nlows from C.\nWe deﬁne three tasks (also see Table 1):\n1. proof (inc. QA): CQ →AP: Given C and\nhypothesis fact Q, what is the truth A and\nproof P (if any) of Q?\n2. enumeration: C →I1, ..., In: Which Ii fol-\nlow from C?\n3. abduction(restricted form) CQ →fm: Which\nextra fact fm will make Q true given C?\nWe reuse (and add to) the RuleTaker datasets for\nour work, which include all ﬁve elements above.\nAn example of a RuleTaker theory (facts and rules),\na query, and a proof generated by ProofWriter are\nshown in Figure 1. Facts and rules are English\nstatements, and implications are English statements\nthat logically follow from those facts and rules.\nThe original datasets were generated from synthetic\nlogic programs and their implications, using natural\nlanguage patterns to produce the English forms.\n3.2\nSemantics\nFollowing prior work, we adopt the semantics of\nDatalog (Ceri et al., 1989): A fact is true if it is ei-\nther known (i.e., explicitly stated in the context C),\nor (recursively) is the conclusion of a rule whose\nconditions are true (is “supported”). For handling\nnegation, we use two alternative Datalog semantics:\nThe ﬁrst, following prior work, makes the closed-\nworld assumption (CWA) and uses negation as fail-\nure (NAF), so that any fact not provable is assumed\nfalse. Under this semantics, negated facts and neg-\native rule conclusions are not allowed (redundant\nunder the CWA). The second makes an open-world\nassumption (OWA), and does allow negative facts\nand rule conclusions. Under this semantics, a third\ntruth value Unknown is also possible.\n3.3", "stderr": "", "interrupted": false, "isImage": false, "noOutputExpected": false}

Olausson*1\nAlex Gu*1\nBenjamin Lipkin*2\nCedegao E. Zhang*2\nArmando Solar-Lezama1\nJoshua B. Tenenbaum1,2\nRoger Levy2\n{theoxo, gua, lipkinb, cedzhang}@mit.edu\n1MIT CSAIL 2MIT BCS\n∗Equal contribution.\nAbstract\nLogical reasoning, i.e., deductively inferring\nthe truth value of a conclusion from a set of\npremises, is an important task for artificial in-\ntelligence with wide potential impacts on sci-\nence, mathematics, and society. While many\nprompting-based strategies have been proposed\nto enable Large Language Models (LLMs) to\ndo such reasoning more effectively, they still\nappear unsatisfactory, often failing in subtle\nand unpredictable ways.\nIn this work, we\ninvestigate the validity of instead reformulat-\ning such tasks as modular neurosymbolic pro-\ngramming, which we call LINC: Logical In-\nference via Neurosymbolic Computation. In\nLINC, the LLM acts as a semantic parser, trans-\nlating premises and conclusions from natural\nlanguage to expressions in first-order logic.\nThese expressions are then offloaded to an ex-\nternal theorem prover, which symbolically per-\nforms deductive inference. Leveraging this ap-\nproach, we observe significant performance\ngains on FOLIO and a balanced subset of\nProofWriter for three different models in nearly\nall experimental conditions we evaluate. On\nProofWriter, augmenting the comparatively\nsmall open-source StarCoder+ (15.5B parame-\nters) with LINC even outperforms GPT-3.5 and\nGPT-4 with Chain-of-Thought (CoT) prompt-\ning by an absolute 38% and 10%, respec-\ntively. When used with GPT-4, LINC scores\n26% higher than CoT on ProofWriter while\nperforming comparatively on FOLIO. Further\nanalysis reveals that although both methods on\naverage succeed roughly equally often on this\ndataset, they exhibit distinct and complemen-\ntary failure modes. We thus provide promising\nevidence for how logical reasoning over natural\nlanguage can be tackled through jointly lever-\naging LLMs alongside symbolic provers. All\ncorresponding code is publicly available.1\n∗Author order randomized; all reserve the right to list\ntheir name first.\n1\nhttps://github.com/benlipkin/linc\n1\nIntroduction\nWidespread adoption of large language models\n(LLMs) such as GPT-3 (Brown et al., 2020), GPT-\n4 (OpenAI, 2023), and PaLM (Chowdhery et al.,\n2022) have led to a series of remarkable successes\nin tasks ranging from text summarization to pro-\ngram synthesis. Some of these successes have en-\ncouraged the hypothesis that such models are able\nto flexibly and systematically reason (Huang and\nChang, 2022), especially when using prompting\nstrategies that explicitly encourage verbalizing in-\ntermediate reasoning steps before generating the\nfinal answer (Nye et al., 2021; Wei et al., 2022;\nKojima et al., 2022; Wang et al., 2023b). However,\nthis reasoning ability appears to be unreliable for\ntasks that require reasoning out of domain (Liang\net al., 2022; Saparov et al., 2023), understanding\nnegation (Anil et al., 2022), and following long\nreasoning chains (Dziri et al., 2023). Furthermore,\nwhile the standard approach of “scaling up” seems\nto improve performance across some reasoning do-\nmains, other domains, e.g., reasoning involving use\nof Modus Tollens, show no such improvements\n(McKenzie et al., 2022). These findings suggest\nthat such models may be relying on approximate\nheuristics based on surface-level statistical patterns\nin reasoning tasks, rather than consistent, gener-\nalizable representations and strategies (Srivastava\net al., 2023; Creswell et al., 2023).\nAt the same time, the ability to accurately and\nsoundly perform logical reasoning is important for\nAI and NLP due to its impact on downstream tasks.\nFor example: retrieval-augmented chatbots may\nbecome more truthful if it can be verified that their\nanswers logically follow from the retrieved facts;\ndata-driven models capable of logical reasoning\nmay speed up progress across mathematics and\nthe sciences through automated theorem proving\nand knowledge discovery; and AI tutoring systems\nwhich ensure internal logical consistency might\n5153\n\nPremise:  \nAll rectangles have four sides.\nPremise:  \nAll four-sided things are shapes.\nConclusion: Are all rectangles shapes?\n<PREMISE> all x. (rectangle(x) -> foursides(x)) </PREMISE>\n<PREMISE> all x. (foursides(x) -> isshape(x)) </PREMISE>\n<CONCLUSION> all x. (rectangle(x) -> isshape(x)) </CONCLUSION>\n<PREMISE> all x. (rectangle(x) -> isshape(x)) </CONCLUSION>\nStep 2:\nLogic\nTheorem\nProver\nTrue\nError\nTrue\nUnknown\nStep 3:\nK-Maj\nVoting\nTrue\nStep 1:\nSemantic\nParser\nInput\nOutput\nSample 1\nSample 2\nSample 3\nSample N\nSample 1\nSample 2\nSample 3\nSample N\nFigure 1: This figure showcases the essence of our approach. Starting from a problem in natural language, in\nStep 1, the LLM semantic parser samples logic formulas expressing estimates of the semantics. It is possible that\nsome of these might contain errors, e.g., the second example shows a syntax error involving an extra parenthesis,\nwhereas the fourth example highlights a semantic error caused by mismatched predicates. In Step 2, these are then\neach offloaded to an automated theorem prover, filtering out syntax errors, and producing labels for the remaining\nsamples. In Step 3, the remaining candidate outputs are passed through a majority-vote sieve to arrive at the best\nestimate for a single output label.\nmake for better educational platforms, teaching\nstudents to think more clearly and rigorously. The\nquestion of how to enable state-of-the-art LLMs to\nbecome more reliable logical reasoners is thus one\nof great importance, with far-reaching implications.\nIn this work,\nwe analyze LINC: Logical\nInference via Neurosymbolic Computation (Fig. 1).\nIn LINC, logical reasoning is tackled through a\nmodular, two-step neurosymbolic process. First,\nthe language model converts the natural language\npremises and desired conclusion into first-order\nlogic (FOL) expressions (Enderton, 2001; Barker-\nPlummer et al., 2011). Second, a symbolic FOL\ntheorem prover algorithmically determines the\ntruth value of the conclusion given the formalized\npremises. In practice, we also incorporate a third\nmajority voting step, which is shown to improve\nperformance. LINC is a natural extension of recent\nwork augmenting lanugage models with symbolic\ntools such as calculators or interpreters (Schick\net al., 2023).\nLINC has a key advantage: the language model\nitself no longer needs to perform any deductive rea-\nsoning, which is offloaded to the theorem prover.\nHowever, there are also clear drawbacks: the for-\nmalization from natural language to first-order\nlogic must perfectly capture all relevant informa-\ntion contained in the premises, and any loss of in-\nformation in the formalization procedure may lead\nthe solver astray, leading to an incorrect conclusion.\nAs it is not clear whether the task of formalization\nis more or less difficult than that of end-to-end nat-\nural language reasoning, our core interest in this\nwork is to compare and contrast our neurosym-\nbolic approach to existing reasoning strategies like\nChain-of-Thought. Our contributions are thus\nthree-fold:\n• First, we propose LINC, a two-stage neurosym-\nbolic approach for logical reasoning tasks\n(Sec. 2).\n• Second, we compare LINC to three baseline\nLLM strategies (Fig. 2), across three mod-\nels (StarCoder+, GPT-3.5, GPT-4) and two\ndatasets (FOLIO and ProofWriter) (Sec. 4).\nWe find that LINC significantly improves per-\nformance over every baseline in all experimen-\ntal conditions except for GPT-4 on FOLIO.\n• Third, we provide a thorough error analysis of\nboth LINC and Chain-of-Thought, identify-\ning three high-level failure modes of each. We\ndiscover that these failure modes are distinct,\nhighlighting the potential for a synergy of the\ntwo methods (Sec. 5).\nOverall, we present strong evidence for the po-\ntential of future neurosymbolic logical reasoning\nsystems based on integrating language models and\ntheorem provers.\n5154\n\n2\nLINC: Logical Inference via\nNeurosymbolic Computation\nOur neurosymbolic approach to end-to-end logical\nreasoning consists of two stages. In the first stage,\nthe LLM acts as a semantic parser, translating NL\nstatements into FOL expressions in our supported\nlogic language. In the second stage, these expres-\nsions are parsed from the text generated by the\nLLM and then get passed to an automated theo-\nrem prover; we use Prover9, a high-performance\nprover widely used in the logic community (Mc-\nCune, 2005–2010). The external solver then ex-\necutes a symbolic deduction algorithm, which ei-\nther returns a value from the set {True, False,\nUncertain} or raises an exception due to improper\nFOL syntax (e.g., if the model fails to balance\nparantheses in the formulae).\nAt its core, the strength of this approach lies in\nthe reformulation of the problem space. End-to-\nend NL-based reasoning allows for operation over\na highly flexible expression space, but leaves the\nLLM with the difficult task of performing explicit\ndeductive inference over expressions in this space.\nUsing LINC, we instead trade off the flexible ex-\npression space of NL for syntactically strict logic\nformulas, allowing us to leverage symbolic algo-\nrithms with provable guarantees that the deductive\nchains will be correct with res", "stderr": "", "interrupted": false, "isImage": false, "noOutputExpected": false}

Motivated by how humans refine their written text, we\nintroduce SELF-REFINE, an approach for improving initial outputs from LLMs\nthrough iterative feedback and refinement. The main idea is to generate an initial\noutput using an LLM; then, the same LLM provides feedback for its output and uses\nit to refine itself, iteratively. SELF-REFINE does not require any supervised training\ndata, additional training, or reinforcement learning, and instead uses a single LLM\nas the generator, refiner, and feedback provider. We evaluate SELF-REFINE across 7\ndiverse tasks, ranging from dialog response generation to mathematical reasoning,\nusing state-of-the-art (GPT-3.5 and GPT-4) LLMs. Across all evaluated tasks,\noutputs generated with SELF-REFINE are preferred by humans and automatic\nmetrics over those generated with the same LLM using conventional one-step\ngeneration, improving by ∼20% absolute on average in task performance. Our work\ndemonstrates that even state-of-the-art LLMs like GPT-4 can be further improved at\ntest-time using our simple, standalone approach.2.\n1\nIntroduction\nAlthough large language models (LLMs) can generate coherent outputs, they often fall short in\naddressing intricate requirements. This mostly includes tasks with multifaceted objectives, such\nas dialogue response generation, or tasks with hard-to-define goals, such as enhancing program\nreadability. In these scenarios, modern LLMs may produce an intelligible initial output, yet may\nbenefit from further iterative refinement—i.e., iteratively mapping a candidate output to an improved\none—to ensure that the desired quality is achieved. Iterative refinement typically involves training\na refinement model that relies on domain-specific data (e.g., Reid and Neubig (2022); Schick et al.\n(2022a); Welleck et al. (2022)). Other approaches that rely on external supervision or reward models\nrequire large training sets or expensive human annotations (Madaan et al., 2021; Ouyang et al., 2022),\nwhich may not always be feasible to obtain. These limitations underscore the need for an effective\nrefinement approach that can be applied to various tasks without requiring extensive supervision.\nIterative self-refinement is a fundamental characteristic of human problem-solving (Simon, 1962;\nFlower and Hayes, 1981; Amabile, 1983). Iterative self-refinement is a process that involves creating\nan initial draft and subsequently refining it based on self-provided feedback. In this paper, we demonstrate that LLMs can provide iterative self-refinement\nwithout additional training, leading to higher-quality outputs on a wide range of tasks.\nWe present SELF-REFINE: an iterative self-refinement algorithm that alternates between two gener-\native steps–FEEDBACK and REFINE. These steps work in tandem to generate high-quality outputs.\nGiven an initial output generated by a model M, we pass it back to the same model M to get\nfeedback. This process is repeated either for a specified number of iterations or until M determines that\nno further refinement is necessary. We release all of our code, which is easily\nextensible to other LLMs. In essence, our results show that even when an LLM cannot generate an\noptimal output on its first try, the LLM can often provide useful feedback and improve its own output\naccordingly. However, they\nhave demonstrated a notable ability to comprehend\ntextual inputs and translate them into formal pro-\ngrams, such as mathematical equations (He-Yueya\net al., 2023) or Python codes (Gao et al., 2023). We\nposit that this capability to formulate problems into\ndifferent languages can be extended to symbolic\nlanguages as well. We leverage the few-shot gener-\nalization ability of LLMs to achieve this. By pro-\nviding the LLM with detailed instructions about the\ngrammar of the symbolic language, alongside a few\ndemonstrations as in-context examples, we observe\nthat LLMs, like InstructGPT (Ouyang et al., 2022b)\nand GPT-4 (OpenAI, 2023), can effectively follow\nthe instructions to identify key entities, facts, and\nrules present in the problem statement, and then\ntranslate these elements into symbolic language\nfollowing our defined grammar.\nSpecifically, we use four different symbolic for-\nmulations to cover four common types of logical\nreasoning problems: deductive reasoning, first-\norder logic reasoning, constraint satisfaction prob-\nlem, and analytical reasoning.\nThese formula-\ntions provide a foundation for translating natu-\nral language-based problem statements. The Prolog logic pro-\ngramming language (Clocksin and Mellish, 2003;\nKörner et al., 2022) is arguably the most prominent\nsymbolic language to describe deductive reasoning\nproblems. We adopt its grammar to represent a\nproblem as facts, rules, and queries.\n• Facts: a fact F is a simple statement with a\npredicate and a set of arguments, formulated as\nP(a1, · · · , an), where P is the predicate name and\neach argument ai can be a variable, entity, num-\nber, or bool. For example, Age(Peter, 31) means\n“Peter’s age is 31”, and MadeOfIron(Nails, True)\nrepresents the fact “Nails are made of iron”.\n• Rules: rules are written in the form of clauses:\nF1∧· · ·∧Fm →Fm+1∧· · ·∧Fn, where each Fi is\na fact and the rule means “if the facts F1, · · · , Fm\nare true, then the facts Fm+1 · · · Fn are also...\n--\n23158:...pting it with the erroneous logic form, the\nsolver’s error message, and a set of demonstrations\nshowing common error cases (e.g., a free variable\nis not bounded to any quantifier in FOL) and their\nremedies. However, existing benchmarks are inadequate in measuring the complex logical reasoning capabilities of a model. We present FOLIO, a human-annotated, logically complex and diverse dataset for reasoning in natural language (NL), equipped with first-order logic (FOL) annotations. FOLIO consists of 1,430 examples (unique conclusions), each paired with one of 487 sets of premises used to deductively reason for the validity of each conclusion. The logical correctness of the premises and conclusions is ensured by their FOL annotations, which are automatically verified by an FOL inference engine. In addition to the main NL reasoning task, NL-FOL pairs in FOLIO constitute a new NL-FOL translation dataset. Our experiments on FOLIO systematically evaluate the FOL reasoning ability of supervised fine-tuning on medium-sized language models. For both NL reasoning and NL-FOL translation, we benchmark multiple state-of-the-art language models. Our results show that a subset of FOLIO presents a challenge for one of the most capable Large Language Model (LLM) publicly available, GPT-4.\n\nFOLIO: Natural Language Reasoning with First-Order Logic\n\n  \n\n\nSimeng Han1 Hailey Schoelkopf1 Yilun Zhao1 Zhenting Qi2 Martin Riddell1 Wenfei Zhou1 James Coady1 David Peng1 Yujie Qiao1 Luke Benson1 Lucy Sun1 Alex Wardle-Solano1 Hannah Szabo1 Ekaterina Zubova1 Matthew Burtell1 Jonat", "stderr": "", "interrupted": false, "isImage": false, "noOutputExpected": false}

{"stdout": "URL: https://arxiv.org/html/2209.00840v2\nType: HTML\nLength: 89810 chars (truncated) (offset: 30000)\n\n--- Content ---\n\nning.\n\n#### HybLogic: hybrid annotation\n\nThe task of generating logically sound stories from scratch for a set of facts is very time-consuming for human writers, where the main challenge is to create complex and varied logical patterns to arrive at a conclusion. To address the problems of solely using manual annotation, we also consider a hybrid approach to facilitate the process. Our hybrid method is based on a common form of logical stories: syllogisms. A syllogism consists of two premises and a single conclusion, and the conclusion states some facts about the entities and categories in the premises.\n\nIn this approach, we first generate logically valid stories, which are templates containing abstract categories and entities, by combining multiple syllogisms into a single story template: the conclusion of one syllogism is used as a premise for the next syllogism. There are 256 logically distinct types of syllogisms and 24 of them are valid (Lehman, [1973](https://arxiv.org/html/2209.00840v2#bib.bib21)). We use various combinations of 24 valid syllogisms. We also add in conjunction, disjunction, and implication. We show an example of the resulting templates in Appendix [B](https://arxiv.org/html/2209.00840v2#A2 \"Appendix B HybLogic Template Example ‣ FOLIO: Natural Language Reasoning with First-Order Logic\"). We then ask human annotators to assign nouns, phrases, or clauses to the abstract entities or categories that reflect real-life scenarios to each template and write logically-valid stories in natural language. The usage of the template is to ensure that we have a set of varied and complex logical stories with multiple conclusions. There are many ways of expressing the same logic template in natural language, and so the generated templates augment, rather than limit, the creativity of humans.\n\n###  3.2 Quality control for NL sentences\n\nTo ensure the highest quality of the dataset, we dedicated considerable attention to the following key aspects of the natural language sentences during the quality control process. \n\n#### Factuality and bias\n\nOur dataset prioritizes realism and factual accuracy, steering clear of biases and stereotypes linked to identity markers like race, ethnicity, gender, sexuality, nationality, class, and religion. Toward these objectives, we manually screened all stories and found that 39.2% of the stories suffer from at least one of these issues. We implemented a detailed protocol to rewrite these stories. The protocol is in Appendix [C](https://arxiv.org/html/2209.00840v2#A3 \"Appendix C Factuality and Bias Elimination Protocol ‣ FOLIO: Natural Language Reasoning with First-Order Logic\").\n\n#### Language quality\n\nApart from grammar, we make sure the sentences in our dataset are highly natural. We also eliminate natural language ambiguity when it is possible. We include rules on eliminating ambiguity in Appendix [D](https://arxiv.org/html/2209.00840v2#A4 \"Appendix D Language Quality Control ‣ FOLIO: Natural Language Reasoning with First-Order Logic\"). Employing these rules effectively reduces the ambiguity of natural language in this reasoning dataset, but incurs the tradeoff of limiting variations in some usage of language. However, we note that there is still sufficient variation in terms of sentence structures and logical structures as shown in Table [1](https://arxiv.org/html/2209.00840v2#S1.T1 \"Table 1 ‣ 1 Introduction ‣ FOLIO: Natural Language Reasoning with First-Order Logic\").\n\n###  3.3 Quality control for FOL formulas\n\nWe adopt the FOL definitions and syntax most widely used in the AI community (Russell and Norvig, [2010](https://arxiv.org/html/2209.00840v2#bib.bib33)). We include more details on the definition of FOL we consider and the FOL modelling convention in Appendix [E](https://arxiv.org/html/2209.00840v2#A5 \"Appendix E First-Order Logic ‣ FOLIO: Natural Language Reasoning with First-Order Logic\") In preliminary investigations, we found that the human-written FOL formulas suffer from FOL consistency issues, which necessitates an additional round of quality control for FOL formulas.\n\n#### FOL consistency\n\nOne NL sentence can be translated into FOL through multiple non-equivalent ways. For example, sometimes additional information inferred from a sentence can be represented in FOL, leading to multiple representations. We therefore design an annotation protocol for FOL translation in order to ensure that our FOL translations are as consistent as possible across all examples in our dataset. We highlight a few important strategies used in the annotation protocol in Appendix [F](https://arxiv.org/html/2209.00840v2#A6 \"Appendix F FOL Annotation Protocol ‣ FOLIO: Natural Language Reasoning with First-Order Logic\").\n\n###  3.4 NL-FOL alignment review\n\nApart from checking whether NL and FOL express equivalent meanings, we also add necessary commonsense knowledge in both the NL and FOL premises. Sometimes humans do not write certain commonsense knowledge in the premises that is required in the FOL reasoning process, which is based solely on the premises given. We add such knowledge as additional premises at this stage. In particular, intrinsic properties of some predicates are required in the FOL reasoning process. For example, \"LocatedIn(x,y)\" should be transitive and \"BeFamily(x,y)\" should be symmetric.\n\n###  3.5 FOL verification\n\nRecognizing that the FOL formula annotations can be error-prone, we verify the syntactic validity and label consistency of FOL formula annotations with an FOL inference engine. We include the details of the FOL inference engine in Appendix [G](https://arxiv.org/html/2209.00840v2#A7 \"Appendix G FOL Inference Engine ‣ FOLIO: Natural Language Reasoning with First-Order Logic\").\n\n###  3.6 Dataset statistics\n\nSource | #Stories | #Premises | #Conclusions | NL | Logic |   \n---|---|---|---|---|---|---  \nVocab | #Words | Complexity | #Depth | AST |   \nWikiLogic | 304 | 1353 | 753 | 3250 | 8.508.508.508.50 | 0 - 14 grade | 1 - 5 | 51 |   \nHybLogic | 183 | 1054 | 682 | 1902 | 11.5211.5211.5211.52 | 0 - 14 grade | 5 - 8 | 25 |   \nTotal | 487 | 2407 | 1435 | 4351 | 9.869.869.869.86 | 0 - 14 grade | 76 | 5-8 |   \n  \nTable 3: Statistics based on different data collection methods of FOLIO. _#Words_ is the average number of words per NL sentence.  01234567890020020020020040040040040011477828544610012926781# Reasoning Depth# ConclusionsWikiHyb Figure 1: Distribution of reasoning depths\n\nWe show basic statistics of FOLIO and demonstrate the abundant vocabulary and logical complexity of FOLIO: Tables [1](https://arxiv.org/html/2209.00840v2#S1.T1 \"Table 1 ‣ 1 Introduction ‣ FOLIO: Natural Language Reasoning with First-Order Logic\"), [3](https://arxiv.org/html/2209.00840v2#S3.T3 \"Table 3 ‣ 3.6 Dataset statistics ‣ 3 FOLIO Corpus Construction ‣ FOLIO: Natural Language Reasoning with First-Order Logic\") and Figure [1](https://arxiv.org/html/2209.00840v2#S3.F1 \"Figure 1 ‣ 3.6 Dataset statistics ‣ 3 FOLIO Corpus Construction ‣ FOLIO: Natural Language Reasoning with First-Order Logic\").\n\n#### Basic statistics\n\nTable [3](https://arxiv.org/html/2209.00840v2#S3.T3 \"Table 3 ‣ 3.6 Dataset statistics ‣ 3 FOLIO Corpus Construction ‣ FOLIO: Natural Language Reasoning with First-Order Logic\") shows that examples based on Wikipedia make up the largest portion of FOLIO, with 304 stories, 1,353 NL and FOL premise pairs, and 753 NL and FOL conclusion pairs. Hybrid annotations consist of 183 stories with 1,054 NL and FOL premise pairs, and 682 NL and FOL conclusion pairs in total.\n\n#### Natural language complexity\n\nWe use the Dale-Chall Readability Formula (Dale and Chall, [1948](https://arxiv.org/html/2209.00840v2#bib.bib11), [1995](https://arxiv.org/html/2209.00840v2#bib.bib12)) to show the text complexity of FOLIO following (Singh et al., [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2209.00840v2#bib.bib37); Arps et al., [2022](https://arxiv.org/html/2209.00840v2#bib.bib1); Wei et al., [2021](https://arxiv.org/html/2209.00840v2#bib.bib51)). We s", "stderr": "", "interrupted": false, "isImage": false, "noOutputExpected": false}

[Packages](/pldoc/doc_for?object=packages)\n\n\n\nAvailability:built-in\n\n**prolog_frame_attribute**(+Frame, +Key, :Value)\n    Obtain information about the local stack frame Frame. Frame is a frame reference as obtained through [prolog_current_frame/1](/pldoc/man?predicate=prolog_current_frame/1), [prolog_trace_interception/4](/pldoc/man?predicate=prolog_trace_interception/4) or this predicate. The key values are described below. \n\n**alternative**\n     Value is unified with an integer reference to the local stack frame in which execution is resumed if the goal associated with Frame fails. Fails if the frame has no alternative frame.\n**has_alternatives**\n     Value is unified with `true` if Frame still is a candidate for backtracking; `false` otherwise.\n**goal**\n     Value is unified with the goal associated with Frame. If the definition module of the active predicate is not the calling context, the goal is represented as `<module>:<goal>`. Do not instantiate variables in this goal unless you **know** what you are doing! Note that the returned term may contain references to the frame and should be discarded before the frame terminates.254The returned term is actually an illegal Prolog term that may hold references from the global to the local stack to preserve the variable names.\n**parent_goal**\n**parent_goal**(-Parent)\n    If Value is instantiated to a callable term, find a frame executing the predicate described by Value and unify the arguments of Value to the goal arguments associated with the frame. This is intended to check the current execution context. The user must ensure the checked parent goal is not removed from the stack due to last-call optimisation and be aware of the slow operation on deeply nested calls. \n\nThe variant `parent_goal(-Parent)` unifies the frame reference of the parent of the found frame with Parent. That allows for finding frames higher up in the stack running the same goal.\n\n**predicate_indicator**\n     Similar to `goal`, but only returning the [<module>:]<name>/<arity> term describing the term, not the actual arguments. It avoids creating an illegal term as `goal` and is used by the library `library(prolog_stack)`.\n**clause**\n     Value is unified with a reference to the currently running clause. Fails if the current goal is associated with a foreign (C) defined predicate. See also [nth_clause/3](/pldoc/man?predicate=nth_clause/3) and [clause_property/2](/pldoc/man?predicate=clause_property/2).\n**level**\n     Value is unified with the recursion level of Frame. The top level frame is at level‘0’.\n**parent**\n     Value is unified with an integer reference to the parent local stack frame of Frame. Fails if Frame is the top frame.\n**context_module**\n     Value is unified with the name of the context module of the environment.\n**top**\n     Value is unified with `true` if Frame is the top Prolog goal from a recursive call back from the foreign language; `false` otherwise.\n**hidden**\n     Value is unified with `true` if the frame is hidden from the user, either because a parent has the hide-childs attribute (all system predicates), or the system has no trace-me attribute.\n**skipped**\n     Value is `true` if this frame was skipped in the debugger.\n**pc**\n     Value is unified with the program pointer saved on behalf of the parent goal if the parent goal is not owned by a foreign predicate or belongs to a compound meta-call (e.g., call((a,b))).\n**argument**(N)\n    Value is unified with the N-th slot of the frame. Argument 1 is the first argument of the goal. Arguments above the arity refer to local variables. Fails silently if N is out of range.\n\nTags are associated to your profile if you are logged in\n\nTags:\n\n\n\n\n**[login](/openid/login?openid.return_to=/pldoc/man?predicate%3Dprolog_frame_attribute%252F3)** to add a new annotation post.\n\n[login](/openid/login?openid.return_to=/pldoc/man?predicate%3Dprolog_frame_attribute%252F3)\n\n[Powered by SWI-Prolog 10.1.8](http://www.swi-prolog.org)", "stderr": "", "interrupted": false, "isImage": false, "noOutputExpected": false}

Flan-T5-Large achieves the highest performance in the fine-tuning setting and the accuracy is 65.7%.\n\nWe show that zero-shot prompting GPT-3.5 achieves better results than few-shot prompting text-davinci-002. Chain-of-thought prompting achieves more than a 4% increase over GPT-4. Self-consistency (SC) improves chain-of-thought prompting by 0.6% percent. Tree-of-thought prompting achieves slightly better result than self-consistency with chain-of-thought prompting. For the results of recent methods developed for logical reasoning, LINC (Olausson et al., [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2209.00840v2#bib.bib29)) achieves around a 9% increase over few-shot prompting GPT-4. SynV measures syntactic validity and EAcc measures the inference engine execution accuracy.\n\n#### NL-FOL translation\n\nTable [5](https://arxiv.org/html/2209.00840v2#S5.T5 \"Table 5 ‣ Logical reasoning ‣ 5.3 Main results ‣ 5 Experiments ‣ FOLIO: Natural Language Reasoning with First-Order Logic\") shows the results of NL-FOL translation. The syntactic validity scores are around 93% with both GPT-3.5-Turbo and GPT-4. This indicates that language models with sufficient scales are good at picking up the patterns for FOL formulas and generating syntactically valid FOL formulas. However, GPT-3.5-Turbo and GPT-4 are not yet good at translating an NL story to a logically or semantically similar FOL counterpart, as indicated by the low inference engine execution accuracy score.\n\n##  6 Error Analysis\n\nBelow we provide analysis of our results and key findings, providing additional insights into our dataset FOLIO and the current capabilities of LLMs in logical reasoning. \n\nModels have higher accuracy on examples with fewer reasoning depths than on those with higher number of reasoing depths We show the accuracy categorized by reasoning depths in Figure [2](https://arxiv.org/html/2209.00840v2#S6.F2 \"Figure 2 ‣ 6 Error Analysis ‣ FOLIO: Natural Language Reasoning with First-Order Logic\"). With few-shot prompting, GPT-3.5 and GPT-4 both perform much better on examples with a 0∼3similar-to030\\sim 30 ∼ 3 reasoning depth, indicating that examples with a 4∼7similar-to474\\sim 74 ∼ 7 reasoning depth pose a challenge to the SoTA LMs. With fine-tuning, RoBERTa has slightly higher performance on test examples with 0∼3similar-to030\\sim 30 ∼ 3 reasoning depth than on those with 4∼7similar-to474\\sim 74 ∼ 7 reasoning depth, but the difference is much smaller. This indicates that fine-tuning on longer and more difficult reasoning chains in the training set can improve model performance on equally-long test example chains. We note that the presence and prevalence of these difficult examples are unique to FOLIO. FOLIO’s unique complexity reveals that current LMs are limited in their ability to extrapolate to longer and more complex reasoning chains, and suggests an avenue for further study.\n\nRoBERTaGPT-3.5GPT-40020202020404040406060606080808080Accuracy (%percent\\%%)# d = 0−3030-30 - 3# d = 4∼7similar-to474\\sim 74 ∼ 7 Figure 2: Accuracies of different models categorized into examples with different reasoning depths. Method | Model | Wiki | Hyb  \n---|---|---|---  \nFine-tuning | RoBERTa-large | 60.71 | 63.48  \nNL Prompting | GPT-3.5-Turbo | 68.88 | 47.70  \n| GPT-4 | 75.43 | 53.10  \nNL-FOL EAcc | GPT-3.5-Turbo | 45.17 | 61.82  \n| GPT-4 | 59.12 | 67.93  \nTable 6: Performance differences on the WikiLogic and HybLogic subset of FOLIO. WikiLogic has more diverse logical structures while HybLogic stories have higher reasoning depths.\n\nModels have higher accuracy on WikiLogic than on HybLogic As shown in Table [6](https://arxiv.org/html/2209.00840v2#S6.T6 \"Table 6 ‣ 6 Error Analysis ‣ FOLIO: Natural Language Reasoning with First-Order Logic\"), in logical reasoning, GPT-3.5 and GPT-4 achieve substantially lower results on HybLogic than on WikiLogic and the result is slightly higher than chance. We hypothesize that this is because HybLogic has high logical complexity that the SoTA LLMs like GPT-4 cannot solve yet. Since the examples in WikiLogic are created from scratch by humans, it is possible that LLMs have seen similar texts with similar logical patterns in the training data. However, fine-tuning RoBERTa-large achieves higher performance on HybLogic than on WikiLogic. This is likely because HybLogic is created from templates and some of the logical patterns can be learned during fine-tuning.\n\nIn NL-FOL translation, performs 10 points better on HybLogic than WikiLogic. This could be because WikiLogic has more distinct and diverse sentence-level logical and language patterns and FOL annotations. WikiLogic has 53 ASTs while HybLogic has 33. Despite being more logically complex on a story level, FOL translations for HybLogic stories have simpler logical structures on a statement level. We include case study for one WikiLogic example and one HybLogic example in Appendix [I](https://arxiv.org/html/2209.00840v2#A9 \"Appendix I Case study ‣ FOLIO: Natural Language Reasoning with First-Order Logic\") and further analysis on model performance in Appendix [J](https://arxiv.org/html/2209.00840v2#A10 \"Appendix J Model Performance Analysis ‣ FOLIO: Natural Language Reasoning with First-Order Logic\").\n\nHuman evaluation on model outputs We conduct human evaluation on the GPT-4 model outputs with wrong truth value predictions. Approximately 65% of the time, the model struggles to construct accurate reasoning chains for complex problems with intricate steps, indicating a limited ability to solve problems with long reasoning chains. In 25% of cases, erroneous derivations occur within certain reasoning steps, highlighting potential inaccuracies and flaws in logical deductions. 5% of conclusions in FOLIO have a complex syntactic structure, posing comprehension challenges for GPT-4. 5% of outputs show that GPT-4 leverage commonsense reasoning to employ spurious shortcuts that lead to the wrong truth value for the conclusion.\n\n###  6.1 Human performance\n\nWe collected truth value annotations of logical reasoning for FOLIO test set from expert and non-expert annotators. Our expert annotators are CS college students familiar with FOL. Non-expert annotators are community college or high school students who have not taken the SAT. Both expert and non-expert annotators are native English speakers. Expert annotations achieve an accuracy of 95.98%percent95.9895.98\\%95.98 % while non-expert annotations achieves 61.82%percent61.8261.82\\%61.82 %, with a gap of 34.16%. This shows that sufficient domain knowledge of FOL is necessary for good performance on FOLIO. The expert and GPT-4 gap is 31.82%percent31.8231.82\\%31.82 %, suggesting significant room for model improvement.\n\n##  7 Conclusion\n\nWe introduced FOLIO, an expert-written dataset for logical reasoning equipped with FOL formulas. The examples in FOLIO are created based on real-world knowledge with natural language. It exhibits a large number of distinct logic patterns and a large vocabulary. Experiments show that FOLIO presents a challenge for one of the most capable Large Language Model publicly available.\n\n##  8 Limitations\n\nDue to the significant effort required for data annotation, we were unable further expand our dataset for investigating how the size of training data affects the performance of fine-tuning experiments. We encourage the community to apply our annotation protocol to expand this realistic and complex FOL reasoning story set.\n\n## References\n\n  * Arps et al.
